| Coordinates: [53°54′02″N 27°33′31″E﻿ / ﻿53.90056°N 27.55861°E﻿ / 53.90056; 27.55861](https://geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Minsk&params=53_54_02_N_27_33_31_E_region:BY_type:city(1992862)) ||

**Minsk** ([Belarusian](/wiki/Belarusian_language "Belarusian language"): Мінск [\[mʲinsk\]](/wiki/Help:IPA/Belarusian "Help:IPA/Belarusian"); [Russian](/wiki/Russian_language "Russian language"): Минск [\[mʲinsk\]](/wiki/Help:IPA/Russian "Help:IPA/Russian")) is the capital and largest city of [Belarus](/wiki/Belarus "Belarus"), located on the [Svislach](/wiki/Svislach_(Berezina) "Svislach (Berezina)") and the now subterranean [Niamiha](/wiki/Nyamiha "Nyamiha") rivers. As the capital, Minsk has a special administrative status in Belarus and is the administrative centre of [Minsk region](/wiki/Minsk_region "Minsk region") and [Minsk district](/wiki/Minsk_district "Minsk district"). As of 2024,^[\[update\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit)^ it has a population of about two million,^[\[2\]](#cite_note-pop-2)^ making Minsk the [11th-most populous city in Europe](/wiki/Largest_cities_in_Europe "Largest cities in Europe"). Minsk is one of the administrative capitals of the [Commonwealth of Independent States](/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States "Commonwealth of Independent States") (CIS) and the [Eurasian Economic Union](/wiki/Eurasian_Economic_Union "Eurasian Economic Union") (EAEU).{#cite_ref-pop_2-1}

First mentioned in 1067, Minsk became the capital of the [Principality of Minsk](/wiki/Principality_of_Minsk "Principality of Minsk"), an [appanage](/wiki/Appanage "Appanage") of the [Principality of Polotsk](/wiki/Principality_of_Polotsk "Principality of Polotsk"), before being annexed by the [Grand Duchy of Lithuania](/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania "Grand Duchy of Lithuania") in 1242. It received [town privileges](/wiki/Town_privileges "Town privileges") in 1499.^[\[4\]](#cite_note-4)^ From 1569, it was the capital of [Minsk Voivodeship](/wiki/Minsk_Voivodeship "Minsk Voivodeship"), an administrative division of the [Polish--Lithuanian Commonwealth](/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth "Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth"). It was part of the territories annexed by the [Russian Empire](/wiki/Russian_Empire "Russian Empire") in 1793, as a consequence of the [Second Partition of Poland](/wiki/Second_Partition_of_Poland "Second Partition of Poland"). From 1919 to 1991, after the [Russian Revolution](/wiki/Russian_Revolution "Russian Revolution"), Minsk was the capital of the [Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic](/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic "Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic"), which became a [republic](/wiki/Republics_of_the_Soviet_Union "Republics of the Soviet Union") of the [Soviet Union](/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") in 1922. Following the [dissolution of the Soviet Union](/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union"), Minsk became the capital of the newly independent Republic of Belarus.{#cite_ref-4}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Minsk%2C_June_2011.jpg/220px-Minsk%2C_June_2011.jpg)](/wiki/File:Minsk,_June_2011.jpg) [Trajeckaje pradmiescie](/wiki/Trinity_Suburb "Trinity Suburb")  

Etymology and historical names {#Etymology_and_historical_names}
----------------------------------------------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Etymology and historical names")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Church_of_Saints_Simon_and_Helena%2C_15%2C_Soviet_str.%2C_Minsk_-_the_capital_of_Republic_of_Belarus_04.JPG/220px-Church_of_Saints_Simon_and_Helena%2C_15%2C_Soviet_str.%2C_Minsk_-_the_capital_of_Republic_of_Belarus_04.JPG)](/wiki/File:Church_of_Saints_Simon_and_Helena,_15,_Soviet_str.,_Minsk_-_the_capital_of_Republic_of_Belarus_04.JPG) [Independence Square](/wiki/Independence_Square,_Minsk "Independence Square, Minsk") in the centre of Minsk

The [Old East Slavic](/wiki/Old_East_Slavic "Old East Slavic") name of the town was Мѣньскъ (i.e. *Měnsk* \< [Early Proto-Slavic](/wiki/Proto-Slavic_language "Proto-Slavic language") or [Late Indo-European](/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language "Proto-Indo-European language") *Mēnĭskŭ* ), derived from a river name *Měn* (\< *Mēnŭ* ).^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^ The resulting^\[*[clarification needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify "Wikipedia:Please clarify")*\]^ form of the name, *Minsk* (spelled either Минскъ or Мѣнскъ), was taken over both in Russian (modern spelling: Минск) and Polish (*Mińsk* ), and under the [influence of Russian](/wiki/Russification_of_Belarus "Russification of Belarus") this form also became official in Belarusian. The direct continuation of the name in Belarusian is *Miensk* ([Менск](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA#Belarusian "wikt:Менск"),
IPA: [\[ˈmʲɛnsk\]](/wiki/Help:IPA/Belarusian "Help:IPA/Belarusian")),^[\[5\]](#cite_note-5)^ which some Belarusian-speakers continue to use as their preferred name for the city.^[\[6\]](#cite_note-6)^{#cite_ref-6}

When Belarus was under Polish rule, the names *Mińsk Litewski* ("Minsk of [Lithuania](/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania "Grand Duchy of Lithuania")") and *Mińsk Białoruski* ("Minsk of Belarus") were used to differentiate this place name from [Mińsk Mazowiecki](/wiki/Mi%C5%84sk_Mazowiecki "Mińsk Mazowiecki") 'Minsk in [Masovia](/wiki/Masovia "Masovia")'. In modern Polish, *Mińsk* without an attribute usually refers to the city in Belarus, which is about 50 times bigger than Mińsk Mazowiecki; (cf. [Brest-Litovsk](/wiki/Brest,_Belarus "Brest, Belarus") and [Brześć Kujawski](/wiki/Brze%C5%9B%C4%87_Kujawski "Brześć Kujawski") for a similar case).^[\[7\]](#cite_note-7)^{#cite_ref-7}  

History {#History}
------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: History")\]  
Main article: [History of Minsk](/wiki/History_of_Minsk "History of Minsk")  
For a chronological guide, see [Timeline of Minsk](/wiki/Timeline_of_Minsk "Timeline of Minsk").  

### Early history {#Early_history}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Early history")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B010.JPG/180px-%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B010.JPG)](/wiki/File:%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B010.JPG) The Saviour Church, built under the [Polish--Lithuanian Commonwealth](/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth "Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth") in 1577, is part of an archaeological preservation in [Zaslavl](/wiki/Zaslawye "Zaslawye"), 23 km (14 mi) northwest of Minsk.

The [Svislach River](/wiki/Svislach_(Berezina) "Svislach (Berezina)") valley was the settlement boundary between two early East Slavic tribes -- the [Krivichs](/wiki/Krivichs "Krivichs") and [Dregovichs](/wiki/Dregovichs "Dregovichs"). By 980, the area was incorporated into the [early medieval](/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages "Early Middle Ages") [Principality of Polotsk](/wiki/Principality_of_Polotsk "Principality of Polotsk"), one of the earliest East Slavic principalities of [Kievan Rus'](/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27 "Kievan Rus'"). Minsk was first mentioned in the name form *Měneskъ* (Мѣнескъ) in the *[Primary Chronicle](/wiki/Primary_Chronicle "Primary Chronicle")* for the year 1067 in association with the [Battle on the River Nemiga](/wiki/Battle_on_the_Nemiga_River "Battle on the Nemiga River").^[\[8\]](#cite_note-8)^ 1067 is now widely accepted as the founding year of Minsk. City authorities consider the date of 3 March 1067 to be the exact founding date of the city,^[\[9\]](#cite_note-9)^ though the town (by then fortified by wooden walls) had certainly existed for some time by then. The origin of the name is unknown but there are several theories.^[\[10\]](#cite_note-10)^{#cite_ref-10}

In the early 12th century, the [Principality of Polotsk](/wiki/Principality_of_Polotsk "Principality of Polotsk") disintegrated into smaller fiefs. The [Principality of Minsk](/wiki/Principality_of_Minsk "Principality of Minsk") was established by one of the [Polotsk](/wiki/Polotsk "Polotsk") dynasty princes. In 1129, the Principality of Minsk was annexed by [Kiev](/wiki/Principality_of_Kiev "Principality of Kiev"), the dominant principality of [Kievan Rus'](/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27 "Kievan Rus'"); however in 1146 the Polotsk dynasty regained control of the principality. By 1150, Minsk rivalled Polotsk as the major city in the former Principality of Polotsk. The princes of Minsk and Polotsk were engaged in years of struggle trying to unite all lands previously under the rule of Polotsk.^[\[11\]](#cite_note-11)^{#cite_ref-11}  

### Late Middle Ages {#Late_Middle_Ages}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Late Middle Ages")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Miensk._%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA_%281772%29_%282%29.jpg/220px-Miensk._%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA_%281772%29_%282%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Miensk._%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA_(1772)_(2).jpg) Minsk in 1772

Minsk escaped the [Mongol invasion of Rus](/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Rus%27 "Mongol invasion of Rus'") in 1237--1239. In 1242, Minsk became a part of the expanding [Grand Duchy of Lithuania](/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania "Grand Duchy of Lithuania"). It joined peacefully and local elites enjoyed high rank in the society of the Grand Duchy. In 1413, the [Grand Duchy of Lithuania](/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania "Grand Duchy of Lithuania") and [Kingdom of Poland](/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1385%E2%80%931569) "Kingdom of Poland (1385–1569)") entered into a union. Minsk became the centre of [Minsk Voivodship](/wiki/Minsk_Voivodeship "Minsk Voivodeship") (province). In 1441, as [Grand Duke of Lithuania](/wiki/Grand_Duke_of_Lithuania "Grand Duke of Lithuania"), [Casimir IV](/wiki/Casimir_IV_Jagiellon "Casimir IV Jagiellon") included Minsk in a list of cities enjoying certain privileges, and in 1499, during the reign of his son, [Alexander I Jagiellon](/wiki/Alexander_Jagiellon "Alexander Jagiellon"), Minsk received [town privileges](/wiki/Town_privileges "Town privileges") under [Magdeburg law](/wiki/Magdeburg_rights "Magdeburg rights"). In 1569, after the [Union of Lublin](/wiki/Union_of_Lublin "Union of Lublin"), the [Grand Duchy of Lithuania](/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania "Grand Duchy of Lithuania") and the [Kingdom of Poland](/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1385%E2%80%931569) "Kingdom of Poland (1385–1569)") merged into a single state, the [Polish--Lithuanian Commonwealth](/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth "Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth").^[\[12\]](#cite_note-12)^{#cite_ref-12}

By the middle of the 16th century, Minsk was an important economic and cultural centre in the [Polish--Lithuanian Commonwealth](/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth "Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth"). It was also an important centre for the [Eastern Orthodox Church](/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church "Eastern Orthodox Church"). Following the [Union of Brest](/wiki/Union_of_Brest "Union of Brest"), both the [Eastern Catholic Churches](/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches "Eastern Catholic Churches") and the Roman Catholic Church increased in influence.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^

In 1655, Minsk was conquered by troops of [Tsar Alexei](/wiki/Alexis_of_Russia "Alexis of Russia") of Russia.^[\[13\]](#cite_note-13)^ Russians governed the city until 1660 when it was regained by [John II Casimir](/wiki/John_II_Casimir "John II Casimir"), Grand Duke of Lithuania and King of Poland. By the end of the [Polish-Russian War](/wiki/Russo-Polish_War_(1654%E2%80%931667) "Russo-Polish War (1654–1667)"), Minsk had only about 2,000 residents and just 300 houses. The second wave of devastation occurred during the [Great Northern War](/wiki/Great_Northern_War "Great Northern War"), when Minsk was occupied in 1708 and 1709 by the army of [Charles XII of Sweden](/wiki/Charles_XII_of_Sweden "Charles XII of Sweden") and then by the army of [Peter the Great](/wiki/Peter_I_of_Russia "Peter I of Russia"). ^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^ The last decades of the Polish rule involved decline or very slow development, since Minsk had become a small provincial town of little economic or military significance.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^{#cite_ref-13}  

### Russian rule {#Russian_rule}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Russian rule")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Stara%C5%BEo%C5%ADka%2C_carkva._%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%BE%D1%9E%D0%BA%D0%B0%2C_%D1%86%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0_%282019%29.jpg/220px-Stara%C5%BEo%C5%ADka%2C_carkva._%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%BE%D1%9E%D0%BA%D0%B0%2C_%D1%86%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0_%282019%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Stara%C5%BEo%C5%ADka,_carkva._%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%BE%D1%9E%D0%BA%D0%B0,_%D1%86%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0_(2019).jpg) Orthodox church of St. Mary Magdalene (built in 1847)

Minsk was annexed by Russia in 1793 as a consequence of the [Second Partition of Poland](/wiki/Second_Partition_of_Poland "Second Partition of Poland").^[\[14\]](#cite_note-14)^^[\[15\]](#cite_note-15)^ In 1796, it became the centre of the [Minsk Governorate](/wiki/Minsk_Governorate "Minsk Governorate"). All of the initial [street names](/wiki/Street_or_road_name "Street or road name") were replaced by Russian names, though the spelling of the city's name remained unchanged. It was briefly occupied by the [Grande Armée](/wiki/Grande_Arm%C3%A9e "Grande Armée") during [French invasion of Russia](/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia "French invasion of Russia") in 1812.^[\[16\]](#cite_note-16)^{#cite_ref-16}

Throughout the 19th century, the city continued to grow and significantly improve. In the 1830s, major streets and squares of Minsk were cobbled and paved. A first public library was opened in 1836, and a fire brigade was put into operation in 1837. In 1838, the first local newspaper, *Minskiye gubernskiye vedomosti* ("Minsk province news") went into circulation. The first theatre was established in 1844. By 1860, Minsk was an important trading city with a population of 27,000. There was a construction boom that led to the building of two- and three-story brick and stone houses in *Upper Town* .^[\[17\]](#cite_note-:0-17)^^[\[18\]](#cite_note-18)^{#cite_ref-18}

Minsk's development was boosted by improvements in transportation. In 1846, the Moscow-[Warsaw](/wiki/Warsaw "Warsaw") road was laid through Minsk. In 1871, a railway link between Moscow and Warsaw ran via Minsk, and in 1873, a new [railway from Romny in Ukraine to the Baltic Sea port of Libava](/wiki/Libau%E2%80%93Romny_Railway "Libau–Romny Railway") ([Liepāja](/wiki/Liep%C4%81ja "Liepāja")) was also constructed. Thus Minsk became an important rail junction and a manufacturing hub. A [municipal water supply](/wiki/Water_supply_network "Water supply network") was introduced in 1872, the telephone in 1890, the horse tram in 1892, and the first power generator in 1894. By 1900, Minsk had 58 factories employing 3,000 workers. The city also boasted theatres, cinemas, newspapers, schools and colleges, as well as numerous monasteries, churches, synagogues, and a mosque. According to the 1897 [Russian census](/wiki/Russian_Empire_Census "Russian Empire Census"), the city had 91,494 inhabitants, with some 47,561 Jews constituting more than half of the city population.^[\[17\]](#cite_note-:0-17)^^[\[19\]](#cite_note-19)^{#cite_ref-19}  

### 20th century {#20th_century}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: 20th century")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Minsk_1912.jpg/220px-Minsk_1912.jpg)](/wiki/File:Minsk_1912.jpg) The [Jesuit Collegium](/wiki/Jesuit_College_in_Minsk "Jesuit College in Minsk") in 1912 [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Miensk%2C_BNR._%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%2C_%D0%91%D0%9D%D0%A0_%281918%29.jpg/220px-Miensk%2C_BNR._%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%2C_%D0%91%D0%9D%D0%A0_%281918%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Miensk,_BNR._%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA,_%D0%91%D0%9D%D0%A0_(1918).jpg) [Belarusian national flag](/wiki/White-red-white_flag "White-red-white flag") over the building of the People's Secretariat of the Belarusian People's Republic [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Kurapaty_1989_meeting.jpg/220px-Kurapaty_1989_meeting.jpg)](/wiki/File:Kurapaty_1989_meeting.jpg) Meeting in the [Kurapaty](/wiki/Kurapaty "Kurapaty") woods, 1989, where between 1937 and 1941 from 30,000 to 250,000 Belarusian [intelligentsia](/wiki/Intelligentsia "Intelligentsia") members were murdered by the [NKVD](/wiki/NKVD "NKVD") during the [Great Purge](/wiki/Great_Purge "Great Purge")

In the early years of the 20th century, Minsk was a major centre for the worker's movement in Belarus. The [1st Congress of the Russian Social Democratic Labour Party](/wiki/1st_Congress_of_the_Russian_Social_Democratic_Labour_Party "1st Congress of the Russian Social Democratic Labour Party"), the forerunner to the [Bolsheviks](/wiki/Bolshevik "Bolshevik") and eventually the [CPSU](/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union "Communist Party of the Soviet Union"), was held there in 1898. It was also one of the major centres of the [Belarusian national revival](/wiki/Belarusian_national_revival "Belarusian national revival"), alongside [Vilnius](/wiki/Vilnius "Vilnius"). However, the [First World War](/wiki/First_World_War "First World War") significantly affected the development of Minsk. By 1915, Minsk was a battlefront city. Some factories were closed down, and residents began evacuating to the east. Minsk became the headquarters of [the Western Front](/wiki/Western_Front_(Soviet_Union) "Western Front (Soviet Union)") of the Russian army and also housed military hospitals and [military supply](/wiki/Military_logistics "Military logistics") bases.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^

The [Russian Revolution](/wiki/Russian_Revolution_(1917) "Russian Revolution (1917)") had an immediate effect in Minsk. A Workers' Soviet was established in Minsk in October 1917, drawing much of its support from disaffected soldiers and workers. After the [Treaty of Brest-Litovsk](/wiki/Treaty_of_Brest-Litovsk_(Russia%E2%80%93Central_Powers) "Treaty of Brest-Litovsk (Russia–Central Powers)"), [German forces occupied Minsk](/wiki/Belarusian-Polish_administration_of_Minsk "Belarusian-Polish administration of Minsk") on 21 February 1918.^[\[20\]](#cite_note-20)^ On 25 March 1918, Minsk was proclaimed the capital of the [Belarusian People's Republic](/wiki/Belarusian_People%27s_Republic "Belarusian People's Republic"). The republic was short-lived; in December 1918, Minsk was taken over by the [Red Army](/wiki/Red_Army "Red Army"). In January 1919 Minsk was proclaimed the capital of the [Byelorussian SSR](/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic "Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic"), though later in 1919 (see [Operation Minsk](/wiki/Operation_Minsk "Operation Minsk")) and again in 1920, the city was controlled by the [Second Polish Republic](/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic "Second Polish Republic") during the course of the [Polish-Bolshevik War](/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Soviet_War "Polish–Soviet War") between 8 August 1919 and 11 July 1920 and again between 14 October 1920 and 19 March 1921. Under the terms of the [Peace of Riga](/wiki/Peace_of_Riga "Peace of Riga"), Minsk was handed back to the [Russian SFSR](/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic "Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic") and became the capital of the Byelorussian SSR, one of the founding republics of the [Union of Soviet Socialist Republics](/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union").^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^{#cite_ref-20}

A programme of reconstruction and development was begun in 1922. By 1924, there were 29 factories in operation; schools, museums, theatres and libraries were also established. Throughout the 1920s and the 1930s, Minsk saw rapid development with dozens of new factories being built and new schools, colleges, higher education establishments, hospitals, theatres and cinemas being opened. During this period, Minsk was also a centre for the development of Belarusian language and culture.^[\[21\]](#cite_note-21)^{#cite_ref-21}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/RIAN_archive_137811_Children_during_air_raid.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_137811_Children_during_air_raid.jpg)](/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_137811_Children_during_air_raid.jpg) Children during the German bombing of Minsk on 24 June 1941

Before the [Second World War](/wiki/Second_World_War "Second World War"), Minsk had a population of 300,000 people. The Germans captured Minsk in the [Battle of Białystok--Minsk](/wiki/Battle_of_Bia%C5%82ystok%E2%80%93Minsk "Battle of Białystok–Minsk"), as part of [Operation Barbarossa](/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa "Operation Barbarossa"); [after it had been devastated](/wiki/Bombing_of_Minsk_in_World_War_II "Bombing of Minsk in World War II") by the [Luftwaffe](/wiki/Luftwaffe "Luftwaffe"). However, some factories, museums, and tens of thousands of civilians had been evacuated to the east. The Germans designated Minsk the administrative centre of *[Generalbezirk Weißruthenien](/wiki/Generalbezirk_Weissruthenien "Generalbezirk Weissruthenien")* . Communists and sympathisers were killed or imprisoned, both locally and after being transported to Germany. Homes were requisitioned to house invading German forces. Thousands starved as food was seized by the German Army and paid work was scarce. Minsk was the site of one of the largest Nazi-run [ghettos](/wiki/Ghetto "Ghetto") in the Second World War, temporarily housing over 100,000 Jews (see [Minsk Ghetto](/wiki/Minsk_Ghetto "Minsk Ghetto")). Some anti-Soviet residents of Minsk, who hoped that Belarus could regain independence, did support the Germans, especially at the beginning of the occupation, but by 1942, Minsk had become a major centre of the [Soviet partisan](/wiki/Soviet_partisans "Soviet partisans") [resistance movement](/wiki/Resistance_movement "Resistance movement") against the invasion, in what is known as the *[German-Soviet War](/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II) "Eastern Front (World War II)")* . For this role, Minsk was awarded the title *[Hero City](/wiki/Hero_City_(Soviet_Union) "Hero City (Soviet Union)")* in 1974.^[\[22\]](#cite_note-22)^{#cite_ref-22}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-137-1010-37A%2C_Minsk%2C_deutsche_Truppen_vor_modernen_Geb%C3%A4uden.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-137-1010-37A%2C_Minsk%2C_deutsche_Truppen_vor_modernen_Geb%C3%A4uden.jpg)](/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-137-1010-37A,_Minsk,_deutsche_Truppen_vor_modernen_Geb%C3%A4uden.jpg) German troops marching through Minsk

Minsk was recaptured by Soviet troops on 3 July 1944 in [Minsk Offensive](/wiki/Minsk_Offensive "Minsk Offensive") as part of [Operation Bagration](/wiki/Operation_Bagration "Operation Bagration"). The city was the centre of German resistance to the Soviet advance and saw heavy fighting during the first half of 1944. Factories, municipal buildings, [power stations](/wiki/Power_station "Power station"), bridges, most roads, and 80% of the houses were reduced to rubble. In 1944, Minsk's population was reduced to a mere 50,000.^[\[23\]](#cite_note-23)^{#cite_ref-23}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Gates_of_Minsk_%282016%29.jpg/220px-Gates_of_Minsk_%282016%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Gates_of_Minsk_(2016).jpg) Railway Station Square, an example of Stalinist Minsk architecture [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Nacyjanalny_akademi%C4%8Dny_teatar_imia_Janki_Kupa%C5%82y.JPG/220px-Nacyjanalny_akademi%C4%8Dny_teatar_imia_Janki_Kupa%C5%82y.JPG)](/wiki/File:Nacyjanalny_akademi%C4%8Dny_teatar_imia_Janki_Kupa%C5%82y.JPG) [Janka Kupala National Theatre](/wiki/Janka_Kupala_National_Theatre "Janka Kupala National Theatre")

The historical centre was replaced in the 1940s and 1950s by [Stalinist architecture](/wiki/Stalinist_architecture "Stalinist architecture"), which favoured grand buildings, broad avenues and wide squares. Subsequently, the city grew rapidly as a result of massive industrialisation. Since the 1960s Minsk's population has also grown apace, reaching 1 million in 1972 and 1.5 million in 1986. Construction of [Minsk Metro](/wiki/Minsk_Metro "Minsk Metro") began on 16 June 1977, and the system was opened to the public on 30 June 1984, becoming the ninth metro system in the Soviet Union. The rapid population growth was primarily driven by mass migration of young, unskilled workers from rural areas of Belarus, as well as by migration of [skilled workers](/wiki/Skilled_worker "Skilled worker") from other parts of the [Soviet Union](/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union").^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^^[\[24\]](#cite_note-24)^ To house the expanding population, Minsk spread beyond its historical boundaries. Its surrounding villages were absorbed and rebuilt as *mikroraions* , districts of high-density apartment housing.^[\[25\]](#cite_note-25)^{#cite_ref-25}  

### Recent developments {#Recent_developments}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=7 "Edit section: Recent developments")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Niezalie%C5%BEnasci_avenue_%28Minsk%29%2C_November_2019_p8.jpg/220px-Niezalie%C5%BEnasci_avenue_%28Minsk%29%2C_November_2019_p8.jpg)](/wiki/File:Niezalie%C5%BEnasci_avenue_(Minsk),_November_2019_p8.jpg) [Independence Avenue](/wiki/Independence_Avenue_(Minsk) "Independence Avenue (Minsk)") (Initial part of avenue candidates for inclusion in [World Heritage Site](/wiki/World_Heritage_Site "World Heritage Site"))

Throughout the 1990s, after the fall of Communism, the city continued to change. As the capital of a newly independent country, Minsk quickly acquired the attributes of a major city. Embassies were opened, and a number of Soviet administrative buildings became government centres. During the early and mid-1990s, Minsk was hit by an [economic crisis](/wiki/Financial_crisis "Financial crisis") and many development projects were halted, resulting in high unemployment and underemployment. Since the late 1990s, there have been improvements in transport and infrastructure, and a housing boom has been underway since 2002. On the outskirts of Minsk, new *[mikroraions](/wiki/Microdistrict "Microdistrict")* of [residential development](/wiki/Residential_area "Residential area") have been built. Metro lines have been extended, and the road system (including the [Minsk BeltWay](/wiki/MKAD_(Minsk) "MKAD (Minsk)")) has been improved. In recent years Minsk has been continuously decentralizing,^[\[26\]](#cite_note-realty1-26)^ with a third line of the [Minsk Metro](/wiki/Minsk_Metro "Minsk Metro") opening in 2020. More development is planned for several areas outside the city centre, while the future of the older neighborhoods is still unclear.^[\[26\]](#cite_note-realty1-26)^{#cite_ref-realty1_26-1}  

Geography {#Geography}
----------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: Geography")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Minsk_city_%28Belarus%29%2C_Sentinel-2_satellite_image%2C_2019-05-19.jpg/220px-Minsk_city_%28Belarus%29%2C_Sentinel-2_satellite_image%2C_2019-05-19.jpg)](/wiki/File:Minsk_city_(Belarus),_Sentinel-2_satellite_image,_2019-05-19.jpg) Minsk satellite photo, 2019

Minsk is located on the southeastern slope of the Minsk Hills, a region of rolling hills running from the southwest (upper reaches of the river [Nioman](/wiki/Neman "Neman")) to the northeast^[\[27\]](#cite_note-27)^-- that is, to Lukomskaye Lake in northwestern [Belarus](/wiki/Belarus "Belarus"). The average altitude above sea level is 220 metres (720 ft). The physical geography of Minsk was shaped over the two [most recent ice ages](/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation "Quaternary glaciation"). The [Svislach River](/wiki/Svislach_(Berezina) "Svislach (Berezina)"), which flows across the city from the northwest to the southeast, is in the *[urstromtal](/wiki/Urstromtal "Urstromtal")* , an ancient river valley formed by water flowing from melting [ice sheets](/wiki/Ice_sheet "Ice sheet") at the end of the last Ice Age. There are six smaller rivers within the city limits, all part of the [Black Sea](/wiki/Black_Sea "Black Sea") basin.{#cite_ref-27}

Minsk is in the area of [mixed forests](/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forests "Temperate broadleaf and mixed forests") typical of most of Belarus. Pinewood and mixed forests border the edge of the city, especially in the north and east. Some of the forests were preserved as parks (for instance, the [Chelyuskinites Park](/wiki/Chelyuskinites_Park "Chelyuskinites Park")) as the city grew.

The city was initially built on the hills, which allowed for defensive fortifications, and the western parts of the city are the most hilly.

In 5 km (3.1 mi) from the northwestern edge of city lies large [Zaslawskaye reservoir](/wiki/Zaslawskaye_reservoir "Zaslawskaye reservoir"), often called the Minsk sea. It is the second largest reservoir in Belarus, constructed in 1956.^[\[28\]](#cite_note-28)^{#cite_ref-28}  

### Climate {#Climate}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Climate")\]

Minsk has a warm summer [humid continental climate](/wiki/Humid_continental_climate "Humid continental climate") ([Köppen *Dfb*](/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification#Dfb/Dwb/Dsb:_Warm_summer_continental_or_hemiboreal_climates "Köppen climate classification")), although its weather is oftentimes unpredictable, given its location between the strong influence of the moist air over the Atlantic Ocean and the dry air over the [Eurasian](/wiki/Eurasia "Eurasia") landmass. Its weather is unstable and tends to change relatively often. The average January temperature is −4.2 °C (24.4 °F), while the average July temperature is 19.1 °C (66.4 °F). The lowest temperature was recorded on 17 January 1940, at −39.1 °C (−38 °F) and the warmest on 8 August 2015 at 35.8 °C (96 °F). Fog is frequent, especially in the autumn and spring. Minsk receives 686 millimetres (27.0 in) of [precipitation](/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation") annually, of which one-third falls during the cold period of the year (as snow or rain) and two-thirds during the warm period. Throughout the year, winds are generally westerly or northwesterly, bringing cool and moist air from the Atlantic.  
{#cite_ref-pogoda.ru.net_29-0}{#cite_ref-NOAA_31-0}

### Ecological situation {#Ecological_situation}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Ecological situation")\]

The ecological situation is monitored by Republican Centre of Radioactive and Environmental Control.^[\[32\]](#cite_note-eco.1-32)^{#cite_ref-eco.1_32-0}
[![Svislač river in autumn.](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Minsk_after_snow_and_freezing_fog_%288216065783%29_%282%29.jpg/220px-Minsk_after_snow_and_freezing_fog_%288216065783%29_%282%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Minsk_after_snow_and_freezing_fog_(8216065783)_(2).jpg) The [Svislač River](/wiki/Svislach_(Berezina) "Svislach (Berezina)") in autumn

During 2003--2008 the overall weight of contaminants increased from 186,000 to 247,400 [tons](/wiki/Tonne "Tonne").^[\[32\]](#cite_note-eco.1-32)^ The change from gas as industrial fuel to [mazut](/wiki/Mazut "Mazut") for financial reasons has worsened the ecological situation.^[\[32\]](#cite_note-eco.1-32)^ However, the majority of overall air pollution is produced by cars.^[\[32\]](#cite_note-eco.1-32)^ Belarusian traffic police *DAI* every year hold operation "Clean Air" to prevent the use of cars with extremely polluting engines.^[\[33\]](#cite_note-33)^ Sometimes the maximum normative concentration of [formaldehyde](/wiki/Formaldehyde "Formaldehyde") and [ammonia](/wiki/Ammonia "Ammonia") in air is exceeded in [Zavodski District](/wiki/Zavodski_District "Zavodski District").^[\[32\]](#cite_note-eco.1-32)^ Other major contaminants are [Chromium](/wiki/Chromium "Chromium")-VI and [nitrogen dioxide](/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide "Nitrogen dioxide").^[\[32\]](#cite_note-eco.1-32)^ Zavodski, [Partyzanski](/wiki/Partyzanski_District "Partyzanski District") and [Leninski](/wiki/Leninsky_District,_Belarus "Leninsky District, Belarus") districts, which are located in the southeastern part of Minsk, are the most polluted areas in the city.^[\[34\]](#cite_note-34)^{#cite_ref-34}  

Demographics {#Demographics}
----------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Demographics")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/City_of_Minsk_population_pyramid_in_2022.svg/220px-City_of_Minsk_population_pyramid_in_2022.svg.png)](/wiki/File:City_of_Minsk_population_pyramid_in_2022.svg) City of Minsk population pyramid in 2022 [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Svislach_bank_1.jpg/220px-Svislach_bank_1.jpg)](/wiki/File:Svislach_bank_1.jpg) Apartment buildings in Minsk [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Minsk_city_%28Belarus%29%2C_Sentinel-2_satellite_image%2C_2019-05-19.jpg/220px-Minsk_city_%28Belarus%29%2C_Sentinel-2_satellite_image%2C_2019-05-19.jpg)](/wiki/File:Minsk_city_(Belarus),_Sentinel-2_satellite_image,_2019-05-19.jpg) Minsk agglomeration from space

### Ethnic groups {#Ethnic_groups}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Ethnic groups")\]

During its first centuries, Minsk was a city with a predominantly Early East Slavic population (the forefathers of modern-day [Belarusians](/wiki/Belarusians "Belarusians")). After the 1569 [Polish--Lithuanian union](/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_union "Polish–Lithuanian union"), the city became a destination for migrating Poles (who worked as administrators, clergy, teachers and soldiers) and Jews ([Ashkenazim](/wiki/Ashkenazim "Ashkenazim"), who worked in the retail trade and as craftsmen, as other opportunities were prohibited by discrimination laws). During the last centuries of the [Polish--Lithuanian Commonwealth](/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth "Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth"), many Minsk residents became [polonised](/wiki/Polonization "Polonization"), adopting the language of the dominant Poles and assimilating to its culture.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^

After the second partition of Poland-Lithuania in 1793, Minsk and its larger region became part of the [Russian Empire](/wiki/Russian_Empire "Russian Empire"). The [Russians](/wiki/Russians "Russians") dominated the city's culture as had the Poles in the earlier centuries.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^

At the time of the [1897 census](/wiki/Russian_Empire_Census "Russian Empire Census") under the Russian Empire, Jews were the largest ethnic group in Minsk, at 52% of the population, with 47,500 of the 91,000 residents.^[\[36\]](#cite_note-36)^ Other substantial ethnic groups were Russians (25.5%), Poles (11.4%) and Belarusians (9%). The latter figure may be not accurate, as some local Belarusians were likely counted as Russians. A small traditional community of [Lipka Tatars](/wiki/Lipka_Tatars "Lipka Tatars") had been living in Minsk for centuries.^[\[37\]](#cite_note-37)^^[\[38\]](#cite_note-38)^{#cite_ref-38}

Between the 1880s and 1930s, many Jews, as well as peasants from other backgrounds, emigrated from the city to the United States as part of a [Belarusian diaspora](/wiki/Belarusian_diaspora "Belarusian diaspora").^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/2019_07_02_16_23_IMG_2649.jpg/220px-2019_07_02_16_23_IMG_2649.jpg)](/wiki/File:2019_07_02_16_23_IMG_2649.jpg) Jewish [Holocaust](/wiki/Holocaust "Holocaust") memorial "[The Pit](/wiki/The_Pit_(memorial) "The Pit (memorial)")" in Minsk

The high mortality of the First World War and the Second World War affected the demographics of the city, particularly the destruction of Jews under the [Nazi](/wiki/Nazism "Nazism") occupation of the Second World War. Working through local populations, Germans instituted deportation of Jewish citizens to concentration camps, murdering most of them there. The Jewish community of Minsk suffered catastrophic losses in [the Holocaust](/wiki/The_Holocaust "The Holocaust"). From more than half the population of the city, the percentage of Jews dropped to less than 10% more than ten years after the war. After its limited population peaked in the 1970s, continuing anti-Semitism under the Soviet Union and increasing nationalism in Belarus caused most Jews to emigrate to Israel and western countries in the 1980s; by 1999, less than 1% of the population of Minsk was Jewish.^[\[39\]](#cite_note-39)^{#cite_ref-39}

In the first three decades of the post-war years, the most numerous new residents in Minsk were rural migrants from other parts of Belarus; the proportion of ethnic [Belarusians](/wiki/Belarusians "Belarusians") increased markedly. Numerous skilled [Russians](/wiki/Russians "Russians") and other migrants from other parts of the [Soviet Union](/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") migrated for jobs in the growing manufacturing sector.^[\[40\]](#cite_note-Zimmerman_2004-40)^ In 1959 Belarusians made up 63.3% of the city's residents. Other ethnic groups included Russians (22.8%), Jews (7.8%), Ukrainians (3.6%), Poles (1.1%) and [Tatars](/wiki/Tatars "Tatars") (0.4%). Continued migration from rural Belarus in the 1960s and 1970s changed the ethnic composition further. By 1979 Belarusians made up 68.4% of the city's residents. Other ethnic groups included Russians (22.2%), Jews (3.4%), Ukrainians (3.4%), Poles (1.2%) and Tatars (0.2%).^[\[40\]](#cite_note-Zimmerman_2004-40)^{#cite_ref-Zimmerman_2004_40-1}

According to the 1989 census, 82% percent of Minsk residents have been born in Belarus. Of those, 43% have been born in Minsk and 39% -- in other parts of Belarus. 6.2% of Minsk residents came from regions of western Belarus (Grodno and Brest Regions) and 13% -- from eastern Belarus (Mogilev, Vitebsk and Gomel Regions). 21.4% of residents came from central Belarus (Minsk Region).^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^

According to the 1999 census, Belarusians make up 79.3% of the city's residents. Other ethnic groups include Russians (15.7%), Ukrainians (2.4%), Poles (1.1%) and Jews (0.6%). The Russian and Ukrainian populations of Minsk peaked in the late 1980s (at 325,000 and 55,000 respectively). After the break-up of the [Soviet Union](/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") many of them chose to move to their respective mother countries, although some families had been in Minsk for generations. Another factor in the shifting demographics of the city was the changing self-identification of Minsk residents of mixed ancestry -- in independent Belarus they identify as Belarusians.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^

The [Jewish population](/wiki/Jewish_population "Jewish population") of Minsk peaked in the early 1970s at 50,000 according to official figures; independent estimates put the figure at between 100,000 and 120,000. Beginning in the 1980s, there has been mass-scale emigration to Israel, the US, and Germany. Today only about 10,000 Jews live in Minsk. The traditional minorities of Poles and Tatars have remained at much the same size (17,000 and 3,000 respectively). Rural Poles have migrated from the western part of Belarus to Minsk, and many Tatars have moved to Minsk from [Tatarstan](/wiki/Tatarstan "Tatarstan").^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^

Some more recent [ethnic minority](/wiki/Minority_group "Minority group") communities have developed as a result of immigration. The most prominent are immigrants from the [Caucasus](/wiki/Caucasus "Caucasus") countries -- [Armenians](/wiki/Armenians "Armenians"), [Azerbaijanis](/wiki/Azerbaijani_people "Azerbaijani people") and [Georgians](/wiki/Georgians "Georgians") each numbering about 2,000 to 5,000. They began migrating to Minsk in the 1970s, and more immigrants have joined them since. Many work in the [retail trade](/wiki/Retailing "Retailing") in open-air markets. A small but prominent Arab community has developed in Minsk, primarily represented by recent economic immigrants from [Syria](/wiki/Syria "Syria"), [Lebanon](/wiki/Lebanon "Lebanon"), [Egypt](/wiki/Egypt "Egypt"), [Algeria](/wiki/Algeria "Algeria"), etc. (In many cases, they are graduates of Minsk universities who decide to settle in Belarus and bring over their families). A small community of [Romani](/wiki/Romani_people "Romani people"), numbering about 2,000, are settled in suburbs of north-western and southern Minsk.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^  

### Languages {#Languages}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Languages")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Chinese_sinage%2C_Main_Railway_Station%2C_Minsk%2C_Belarus.jpg/180px-Chinese_sinage%2C_Main_Railway_Station%2C_Minsk%2C_Belarus.jpg)](/wiki/File:Chinese_sinage,_Main_Railway_Station,_Minsk,_Belarus.jpg) Chinese signage, Minsk railway station (2018)

Throughout its history Minsk has been a city of many languages. Initially most of its residents spoke [Ruthenian](/wiki/Ruthenian_language "Ruthenian language") (which later developed into modern [Belarusian](/wiki/Belarusian_language "Belarusian language")). However, after 1569 the [official language](/wiki/Official_language "Official language") was [Polish](/wiki/Dialects_of_Polish#Northern_Borderlands_dialect "Dialects of Polish").^[\[41\]](#cite_note-41)^ In the 19th-century Russian became the official language and by the end of that century it had become the language of administration, schools and newspapers. The Belarusian national revival increased interest in the Belarusian language -- its use has grown since the 1890s, especially among the [intelligentsia](/wiki/Intelligentsia "Intelligentsia"). In the 1920s and early 1930s Belarusian was the major language of Minsk, including use for administration and education (both secondary and tertiary). However, since the late 1930s Russian again began gaining dominance.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^{#cite_ref-41}

A short period of Belarusian national revival in the early 1990s saw a rise in the numbers of Belarusian speakers. However, in 1994 the newly elected president [Alexander Lukashenko](/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko "Alexander Lukashenko") slowly reversed this trend. Most residents of Minsk now use Russian exclusively in their everyday lives at home and at work, although Belarusian is understood as well. Substantial numbers of recent migrants from the rural areas use [Trasyanka](/wiki/Trasianka "Trasianka") (a Russo-Belarusian mixed language) in their everyday lives.^[\[42\]](#cite_note-42)^{#cite_ref-42}  

### Religion {#Religion}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Religion")\]

There are no reliable statistics on the religious affiliations of those living in Minsk, or among the population of Belarus generally. The majority of Christians belong to the [Belarusian Orthodox Church](/wiki/Belarusian_Orthodox_Church "Belarusian Orthodox Church"), which is the exarchate of the [Russian Orthodox Church](/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church "Russian Orthodox Church") in Belarus. There is a significant minority of [Roman Catholics](/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church "Roman Catholic Church").^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/New_synagogue_in_Minsk.jpg/220px-New_synagogue_in_Minsk.jpg)](/wiki/File:New_synagogue_in_Minsk.jpg) New synagogue in Minsk

As of 2006, there are approximately 30 religious communities of various denominations in Minsk.^[\[43\]](#cite_note-43)^^[\[44\]](#cite_note-44)^ The only functioning [monastery](/wiki/Monastery "Monastery") in the city is [St Elisabeth Convent](/wiki/St_Elisabeth_Convent_(Minsk) "St Elisabeth Convent (Minsk)"); its large complex of churches is open to visitors.{#cite_ref-44}  

### Crime {#Crime}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Crime")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/2020_Belarusian_protests_%E2%80%94_Minsk%2C_30_August_p0023.jpg/220px-2020_Belarusian_protests_%E2%80%94_Minsk%2C_30_August_p0023.jpg)](/wiki/File:2020_Belarusian_protests_%E2%80%94_Minsk,_30_August_p0023.jpg) Police during the [2020--21 Belarusian protests](/wiki/2020%E2%80%9321_Belarusian_protests "2020–21 Belarusian protests")

Minsk has the highest crime rate in Belarus -- 193.5 crimes per 10,000 citizens.^[\[45\]](#cite_note-prest.3-45)^^[\[46\]](#cite_note-46)^ 20--25% of all serious crimes in Belarus, 55% of bribes and 67% of mobile phone thefts are committed in Minsk.^[\[45\]](#cite_note-prest.3-45)^^[\[47\]](#cite_note-prest.2-47)^ However, attorney general Grigory Vasilevich stated that the homicide rate in Minsk in 2008 was "relatively fine".^[\[48\]](#cite_note-48)^{#cite_ref-48}

The crime rate grew significantly in 2009 and 2010:^[\[45\]](#cite_note-prest.3-45)^ for example, the number of corruption crimes grew by 36% in 2009 alone.^[\[49\]](#cite_note-49)^ Crime detection level varies from 13% in burglary^[\[50\]](#cite_note-prest.1-50)^ to 92% in homicide^[\[51\]](#cite_note-prest.4-51)^ with an average 40.1%.^[\[52\]](#cite_note-52)^ Many citizens are concerned for their safety at night and the strongest concern was expressed by residents of Chizhovka and Shabany [microdistricts](/wiki/Microdistrict "Microdistrict") (both in [Zavodski District](/wiki/Zavodski_District "Zavodski District")).^[\[51\]](#cite_note-prest.4-51)^{#cite_ref-prest.4_51-1}

The SIZO-1 detention center, IK-1 general prison, and the [KGB](/wiki/State_Security_Committee_of_the_Republic_of_Belarus "State Security Committee of the Republic of Belarus") special jail called "*Amerikanka* " are all located in Minsk. [Alexander Lukashenko](/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko "Alexander Lukashenko")'s rivals in the [2010 presidential election](/wiki/2010_Belarusian_presidential_election "2010 Belarusian presidential election") were imprisoned in the KGB jail^[\[53\]](#cite_note-53)^ along with other prominent politicians and civil activists. [Ales Michalevic](/wiki/Ales_Michalevic "Ales Michalevic"), who was kept in this jail, accused the KGB of using torture.^[\[54\]](#cite_note-54)^^[\[55\]](#cite_note-55)^{#cite_ref-55}

On 15 November 2020, more than 1,000 protesters were arrested during an anti-government protest. Protesters took to the streets in the capital, Minsk, following the death of an opposition activist, [Raman Bandarenka](/wiki/Death_of_Raman_Bandarenka "Death of Raman Bandarenka"). The activist died after allegedly being beaten up by the security forces. The protesters put flowers at the site where he was detained before succumbing to his injuries.^[\[56\]](#cite_note-56)^{#cite_ref-56}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/2020_Belarusian_protests_%E2%80%94_Minsk%2C_30_August_p0017.jpg/220px-2020_Belarusian_protests_%E2%80%94_Minsk%2C_30_August_p0017.jpg)](/wiki/File:2020_Belarusian_protests_%E2%80%94_Minsk,_30_August_p0017.jpg) [2020--21 Belarusian protests](/wiki/2020%E2%80%9321_Belarusian_protests "2020–21 Belarusian protests") --- Minsk, 30 August 2020  

Economy {#Economy}
------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Economy")\]  
See also: [Economy of Belarus](/wiki/Economy_of_Belarus "Economy of Belarus")

Minsk is the [economic capital](/wiki/Capital_city "Capital city") of Belarus. It has developed industrial and services sectors which serve the needs not only of the city, but of the entire nation. Minsk's contributions form nearly 46% of Belarusian budget.^[\[57\]](#cite_note-57)^ According to 2010 results, Minsk paid 15 trillion BYR to state budget while the whole income from all other regions was 20 trillion BYR.^[\[58\]](#cite_note-58)^ In the period January 2013 to October 2013, 70.6% of taxes in the budget of Minsk were paid by non-state enterprises, 26.3% by state enterprises, and 1.8% by individual entrepreneurs. Among the top 10 taxpayers were five oil and gas companies (including two [Gazprom](/wiki/Gazprom "Gazprom")'s and one [Lukoil](/wiki/Lukoil "Lukoil")'s subsidiaries), two mobile network operators ([MTS](/wiki/MTS_(network_provider) "MTS (network provider)") and [A1](/wiki/A1_Belarus "A1 Belarus")), two companies producing alcoholic beverages (Minsk-Kristall and Minsk grape wines factory) and one producer of tobacco goods.^[\[59\]](#cite_note-59)^{#cite_ref-59}

In 2012, [Gross Regional Product](/wiki/Gross_Regional_Product "Gross Regional Product") of Minsk was formed mainly by industry (26.4%), wholesale (19.9%), transportation and communications (12.3%), retail (8.6%) and construction (5.8%).

GRP of Minsk measured in Belarusian rubles was 55 billion (€20 billion) or around 1/3 of Gross domestic product of Belarus.^[\[60\]](#cite_note-60)^{#cite_ref-60}

Minsk city has highest salaries in Belarus. As of December 2023 average gross salary in Minsk was 3,240 BYN per month (\~ US$1,000).^[\[61\]](#cite_note-myfin.by-61)^{#cite_ref-myfin.by_61-0}  

### Industry {#Industry}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Industry")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/3rd_thermal_power_plant_%28Minsk%29_07.jpg/220px-3rd_thermal_power_plant_%28Minsk%29_07.jpg)](/wiki/File:3rd_thermal_power_plant_(Minsk)_07.jpg) Power plant

Minsk is the major industrial centre of Belarus. According to 2012 statistics, Minsk-based companies produced 21.5% of electricity, 76% of trucks, 15.9% of footwear, 89.3% of television sets, 99.3% of washing machines, 30% of chocolate, 27.7% of distilled alcoholic beverages and 19.7% of tobacco goods in Belarus.^[\[62\]](#cite_note-62)^{#cite_ref-62}

Today the city has over 250 factories and plants. Its industrial development started in the 1860s and was facilitated by the railways built in the 1870s. However, much of the industrial infrastructure was destroyed during World War I, especially during World War II. After the last war, the development of the city was linked to the development of industry, especially of [R\&D](/wiki/Research_and_development "Research and development")-intensive sectors (heavy emphasis of R\&D intensive industries in [urban development](/wiki/Urban_planning "Urban planning") in the USSR is known in Western geography as 'Minsk phenomenon').^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^ Minsk was turned into a major production site for trucks, tractors, gears, optical equipment, refrigerators, television sets and radios, bicycles, motorcycles, watches, and metal-processing equipment. Outside machine-building and electronics, Minsk also had textiles, construction materials, food processing, and printing industries. During the Soviet period, the development of the industries was linked to suppliers and markets within the USSR. The break-up of the union in 1991 led to a serious economic meltdown in 1991--1994.^[\[63\]](#cite_note-Intereconomics-63)^{#cite_ref-Intereconomics_63-0}

However, since the adoption of the neo-Keynesean policies under [Alexander Lukashenko](/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko "Alexander Lukashenko")'s government in 1995, much of the gross industrial production was regained.^[\[63\]](#cite_note-Intereconomics-63)^ Unlike many other cities in the CIS and Eastern Europe, Minsk was not heavily de-industrialised in the 1990s. About 40% of the workforce is still employed in the [manufacturing sector](/wiki/Secondary_sector_of_the_economy "Secondary sector of the economy").^[\[63\]](#cite_note-Intereconomics-63)^{#cite_ref-Intereconomics_63-2}

Major industrial employers include:

* [Minsk Tractor Plant](/wiki/Minsk_Tractor_Works "Minsk Tractor Works") -- specialised in manufacturing tractors. Established in 1946 in eastern Minsk, is among major manufacturers of wheeled tractors in the CIS. Employs about 30,000 staff.^[\[64\]](#cite_note-64)^{#cite_ref-64}
* [Minsk Automobile Plant](/wiki/Minsk_Automobile_Plant "Minsk Automobile Plant") -- specialising in producing trucks, buses, and mini-vans. Established in 1944 in south-eastern Minsk, is among major vehicle manufacturers in the CIS.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^
* [Minsk Refrigerator Plant](/wiki/Minsk_Refrigerator_Plant "Minsk Refrigerator Plant") (also known as Atlant) -- specialised in manufacturing household goods, such as refrigerators, freezers, and recently also of [washing machines](/wiki/Washing_machine "Washing machine"). Established in 1959 in the north-west of the city.^[\[65\]](#cite_note-65)^{#cite_ref-65}
* Horizont -- specialised in producing TV-sets, audio and video electronics. Established in 1950 in north-central Minsk.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^

### Unemployment {#Unemployment}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Unemployment")\]

In 2011 official statistics quote unemployment in Minsk at 0.3%.^[\[66\]](#cite_note-nav.2302-66)^ During the 2009 census 5.6% of Minsk residents of employable age called themselves unemployed.^[\[66\]](#cite_note-nav.2302-66)^ The government effectively discourages official unemployment registration because of tiny unemployment benefits and obligatory public works. In 2015--2017 there was an attempt (cancelled in January 2018) to collect the ['free rider tax'](/w/index.php?title=Decree_on_the_prevention_of_social_parasitism&action=edit&redlink=1 "Decree on the prevention of social parasitism (page does not exist)") \[[ru](https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%94%D0%B5%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%BE_%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B4%D1%83%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%B4%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B8_%D1%81%D0%BE%D1%86%D0%B8%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE_%D0%B8%D0%B6%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B2%D0%B0 "ru:Декрет о предупреждении социального иждивенчества")\] taken from those who were unemployed unregistered for more than 6 months.^[\[67\]](#cite_note-67)^{#cite_ref-67}  

Government and administrative divisions {#Government_and_administrative_divisions}
----------------------------------------------------------------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Government and administrative divisions")\]  
Main article: [Administrative divisions of Minsk](/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Minsk "Administrative divisions of Minsk")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/House_of_Representatives_of_Belarus.jpg/220px-House_of_Representatives_of_Belarus.jpg)](/wiki/File:House_of_Representatives_of_Belarus.jpg) [House of Representatives of Belarus](/wiki/House_of_Representatives_of_Belarus "House of Representatives of Belarus") [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/%D0%9C%D1%96%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA._%D0%9F%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%88%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B3%D1%96%2C_%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%96%D0%BA_2020_01.jpg/220px-%D0%9C%D1%96%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA._%D0%9F%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%88%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B3%D1%96%2C_%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%96%D0%BA_2020_01.jpg)](/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D1%96%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA._%D0%9F%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%88%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B3%D1%96,_%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%96%D0%BA_2020_01.jpg) [Victory Square](/wiki/Victory_Square,_Minsk "Victory Square, Minsk")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Minsk_all_districts_color-2011-05-02.png/600px-Minsk_all_districts_color-2011-05-02.png)](/wiki/File:Minsk_all_districts_color-2011-05-02.png)

Minsk is subdivided into nine *[raions](/wiki/Raion "Raion")* (districts):

1.  
   **[Tsentralny](/wiki/Tsentralny_District_(Minsk) "Tsentralny District (Minsk)")** (Belarusian: Цэнтральны, Russian: Центральный), or "Central District"
2.  
   **[Savetski](/wiki/Savetski_District_(Minsk) "Savetski District (Minsk)")** (Belarusian: Савецкі, Russian: Советский, Sovetsky), or "[Soviet](/wiki/Soviet_(council) "Soviet (council)") District"
3.  
   **[Pershamayski](/wiki/Pershamayski_District "Pershamayski District")** (Belarusian: Першамайскі, Russian: Первомайский, Pervomaysky), named after [1 May](/wiki/May_Day "May Day")
4.  
   **[Partyzanski](/wiki/Partyzanski_District_(Minsk) "Partyzanski District (Minsk)")** (Belarusian: Партызанскі, Russian: Партизанский, Partizansky), named after the [Soviet partisans](/wiki/Soviet_partisans "Soviet partisans")
5.  
   **[Zavodski](/wiki/Zavodski_District_(Minsk) "Zavodski District (Minsk)")** (Belarusian: Заводскі, Russian: Заводской, Zavodskoy), or "Factory district" (initially it included major plants, [Minsk Tractor Works](/wiki/Minsk_Tractor_Works "Minsk Tractor Works") (MTZ) and [Minsk Automobile Plant](/wiki/Minsk_Automobile_Plant "Minsk Automobile Plant") (MAZ), later the Partyzanski District with MTZ was split off it)
6.  
   **[Leninski](/wiki/Leninski_District_(Minsk) "Leninski District (Minsk)")** (Belarusian: Ленінскі, Russian: Ленинский, Leninsky), named after [Lenin](/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin "Vladimir Lenin")
7.  
   **[Kastrychnitski](/wiki/Kastrychnitski_District "Kastrychnitski District")** (Belarusian: Кастрычніцкі, Russian: Октябрьский, Oktyabrsky), named after the [October Revolution](/wiki/October_Revolution "October Revolution")
8.  
   **[Maskouski](/wiki/Maskouski_District "Maskouski District")** (Belarusian: Маскоўскі, Russian: Московский, Moskovsky), named after Moscow
9.  
   **[Frunzenski](/wiki/Frunzenski_District_(Minsk) "Frunzenski District (Minsk)")** (Belarusian: Фрунзенскі, Russian: Фрунзенский, Frunzensky), named after [Mikhail Frunze](/wiki/Mikhail_Frunze "Mikhail Frunze")

In addition, a number of residential neighbourhoods are recognised in Minsk, called [microdistricts](/wiki/Microdistrict "Microdistrict"), with no separate administration.  

Culture {#Culture}
------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Culture")\]

Minsk is the major cultural centre of Belarus. Its first theatres and libraries were established in the middle of the 19th century. It presently has 11 theatres and 16 museums, along with 20 cinemas and 139 libraries.^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^  

### Churches {#Churches}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Churches")\]

* The [Orthodox](/wiki/Belarusian_Orthodox_Church "Belarusian Orthodox Church") Cathedral of the Holy Spirit is actually the former church of the [Bernardine](/wiki/Cistercians "Cistercians") convent. It was built in the simplified [Baroque](/wiki/Baroque "Baroque") style in 1642--87 and went through renovations in 1741--46 and 1869.
* The [Cathedral of Saint Mary](/wiki/Cathedral_of_Saint_Virgin_Mary "Cathedral of Saint Virgin Mary") was built by the Jesuits as their principal church in 1700--10, restored in 1951 and 1997; it overlooks the recently restored 18th-century city hall, located on the other side of [Plošča Svabody](/w/index.php?title=Plo%C5%A1%C4%8Da_Svabody&action=edit&redlink=1 "Plošča Svabody (page does not exist)") \[[be](https://be.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%9F%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%88%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%B1%D0%BE%D0%B4%D1%8B_(%D0%9C%D1%96%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA) "be:Плошча Свабоды (Мінск)")\].
* Another two historic churches are the cathedral of [Saint Joseph](/wiki/Saint_Joseph "Saint Joseph"), formerly affiliated with the Bernardine monastery, built in 1644--52 and repaired in 1983, and the fortified church of Sts. Peter and Paul, originally built in the 1620s and recently restored, complete with its flanking twin towers.
* [Church of St. Thomas Aquinas and the Dominican monastery in Minsk](/wiki/Church_of_St._Thomas_Aquinas_and_the_Dominican_monastery_in_Minsk "Church of St. Thomas Aquinas and the Dominican monastery in Minsk") was a [Catholic](/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_Belarus "Catholic Church in Belarus") [monastery](/wiki/Monastery "Monastery") complex founded in the early 17th century, destroyed in 1950. It was built in the [Baroque](/wiki/Baroque "Baroque") style.
* The impressive Neo-Romanesque Roman Catholic [Red Church](/wiki/Church_of_Saints_Simon_and_Helena "Church of Saints Simon and Helena") (Cathedral of Sts. Simeon and Helene) was built in 1906--10 immediately after religious freedoms were proclaimed in Imperial Russia and the tsar allowed dissidents to build their churches;
* The largest church built in the Russian imperial period of the town's history is dedicated to St. [Mary Magdalene](/wiki/Mary_Magdalene "Mary Magdalene");
* [Church of St. Adalbert and Benedictine monastery](/wiki/Church_of_St._Adalbert_and_Benedictine_monastery "Church of St. Adalbert and Benedictine monastery") was a [Roman Catholic](/wiki/Roman_Catholic "Roman Catholic") monastic complex in Minsk originally belonging to the [Benedictine](/wiki/Benedictines "Benedictines") order. Currently, the General Prosecutor's Office of Belarus is located on this site;
* Many Orthodox churches were built after the [dissolution of the USSR](/wiki/History_of_the_Soviet_Union_(1985%E2%80%931991) "History of the Soviet Union (1985–1991)") in a variety of styles, although most remain true to the Neo-Russian idiom. A good example is St. Elisabeth's Convent, founded in 1999.

### Cemeteries {#Cemeteries}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Cemeteries")\]

Tourism {#Tourism}
------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Minsk&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Tourism")\]

There are more than 400 travel agencies in Minsk, about a quarter of them provide agent activity, and most of them are tour operators.^[\[69\]](#cite_note-agencies-regions-69)^^[\[70\]](#cite_note-agencies-activiry-70)^{#cite_ref-agencies-activiry_70-0}
